       Republic in Central America
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       For cities in other countries, see <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rica,_Sinaloa" title="Costa Rica, Sinaloa">Costa Rica, Sinaloa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rica,_Mato_Grosso_do_Sul" title="Costa Rica, Mato Grosso do Sul">Costa Rica, Mato Grosso do Sul</a>.
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rica_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Costa Rica (disambiguation)">Costa Rica (disambiguation)</a>.

      <p><b>Costa Rica</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><small><a href="/wiki/British_English" title="British English">UK</a>: </small><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/ɒ/: 'o' in 'body'">ɒ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span><span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/iː/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">iː</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span>/</a></span></span>, <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><small><a href="/wiki/American_English" title="American English">US</a>: </small><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span>/</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:En-us-Costa_Rica.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20"></a></span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/76/En-us-Costa_Rica.ogg" class="internal" title="En-us-Costa Rica.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span></span>; <small>Spanish:&nbsp;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Spanish" title="Help:IPA/Spanish">[ˈkosta ˈrika]</a></span>; literally "Rich Coast"), officially the <b>Republic of Costa Rica</b> (<a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>: <i lang="es">República de Costa Rica</i>), is a country in <a href="/wiki/Central_America" title="Central America">Central America</a>, bordered by <a href="/wiki/Nicaragua" title="Nicaragua">Nicaragua</a> to the north, the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Sea" title="Caribbean Sea">Caribbean Sea</a> to the northeast, <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">Panama</a> to the southeast, the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a> to the southwest, and <a href="/wiki/Ecuador" title="Ecuador">Ecuador</a> to the south of <a href="/wiki/Cocos_Island" title="Cocos Island">Cocos Island</a>. It has a population of around 5 million<sup id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_2019_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP_2019-6">[6]</a></sup> in a land area of 51,060 square kilometers (19,714 square miles). An estimated 333,980 people live in the capital and largest city, <a href="/wiki/San_Jos%C3%A9,_Costa_Rica" title="San José, Costa Rica">San José</a>, with around 2 million people in the surrounding metropolitan area.<sup id="cite_ref-worldscapitalcities.com_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-worldscapitalcities.com-10">[10]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">sovereign state</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Unitary_state" title="Unitary state">unitary</a> <a href="/wiki/Presidential_system" title="Presidential system">presidential</a> <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Costa_Rica" title="Constitution of Costa Rica">constitutional</a> <a href="/wiki/Republic" title="Republic">republic</a>. It is known for its long-standing and stable democracy, and for its highly educated workforce, most of whom speak English.<sup id="cite_ref-seattletimes.com_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-seattletimes.com-11">[11]</a></sup> The country spends roughly 6.9% of its budget (2016) on education, compared to a global average of 4.4%.<sup id="cite_ref-seattletimes.com_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-seattletimes.com-11">[11]</a></sup> Its economy, once heavily dependent on agriculture, has diversified to include sectors such as finance, corporate services for foreign companies, pharmaceuticals, and <a href="/wiki/Ecotourism" title="Ecotourism">ecotourism</a>. Many foreign manufacturing and services companies operate in Costa Rica's <a href="/wiki/Free-trade_zone" title="Free-trade zone">Free Trade Zones</a> (FTZ) where they benefit from investment and tax incentives.<sup id="cite_ref-cinde.org_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cinde.org-12">[12]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Costa Rica was sparsely inhabited by <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_Costa_Rica" title="Indigenous peoples of Costa Rica">indigenous peoples</a> before coming under <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">Spanish rule</a> in the 16th century. It remained a peripheral colony of the empire until independence as part of the <a href="/wiki/First_Mexican_Empire" title="First Mexican Empire">First Mexican Empire</a>, followed by membership in the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Republic_of_Central_America" title="Federal Republic of Central America">Federal Republic of Central America</a>, from which it formally declared independence in 1847. Following the brief <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rican_Civil_War" title="Costa Rican Civil War">Costa Rican Civil War</a> in 1948, it permanently <a href="/wiki/Public_Force_of_Costa_Rica" title="Public Force of Costa Rica">abolished its army</a> in 1949, becoming one of only a few sovereign <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_without_armed_forces" title="List of countries without armed forces">nations without a standing army</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The country has consistently performed favorably in the <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">Human Development Index</a> (HDI), placing 62nd in the world as of 2019<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Costa_Rica&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, and fifth in Latin America.<sup id="cite_ref-UNHDR_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNHDR-9">[9]</a></sup> It has also been cited by the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Development_Programme" title="United Nations Development Programme">United Nations Development Programme</a> (UNDP) as having attained much higher <a href="/wiki/Human_development_(economics)" title="Human development (economics)">human development</a> than other countries at the same income levels, with a better record on human development and inequality than the median of the region.<sup id="cite_ref-HDI10_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HDI10-16">[16]</a></sup> It performs also well on comparisons of the state of democracy, press freedom and subjective happiness. It has the 7th freest press according to the <a href="/wiki/Press_Freedom_Index" title="Press Freedom Index">Press Freedom Index</a>, it is the 37th most democratic country according to the <a href="/wiki/Freedom_in_the_World" title="Freedom in the World">Freedom in the World</a> index and it is the 12th happiest country in the <a href="/wiki/World_Happiness_Report" title="World Happiness Report">World Happiness Report</a>. </p>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Costa_Rica" title="History of Costa Rica">History of Costa Rica</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Stone_sphere.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Stone_sphere.jpg/220px-Stone_sphere.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="238" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Stone_sphere.jpg/330px-Stone_sphere.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Stone_sphere.jpg/440px-Stone_sphere.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1417" data-file-height="1533"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Stone_sphere.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A stone sphere created by the <a href="/wiki/Diquis" title="Diquis">Diquis</a> culture at the <a href="/wiki/Museo_Nacional_de_Costa_Rica" title="Museo Nacional de Costa Rica">National Museum of Costa Rica</a>. <a href="/wiki/Stone_spheres_of_Costa_Rica" title="Stone spheres of Costa Rica">The sphere</a> is the icon of the country's cultural identity.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-Columbian_period">Pre-Columbian period</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian_history_of_Costa_Rica" title="Pre-Columbian history of Costa Rica">Pre-Columbian history of Costa Rica</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Historians have classified the <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">indigenous people</a> of Costa Rica as belonging to the <a href="/wiki/Intermediate_Area" title="Intermediate Area">Intermediate Area</a>, where the peripheries of the <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican" class="mw-redirect" title="Mesoamerican">Mesoamerican</a> and <a href="/wiki/Andean" class="mw-redirect" title="Andean">Andean</a> native cultures overlapped. More recently, <a href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian_era" title="Pre-Columbian era">pre-Columbian</a> Costa Rica has also been described as part of the <a href="/wiki/Isthmo-Colombian_Area" title="Isthmo-Colombian Area">Isthmo-Colombian Area</a>. </p>
      <p>Stone tools, the oldest evidence of human occupation in Costa Rica, are associated with the arrival of various groups of <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunter-gatherers</a> about 10,000 to 7,000 years <a href="/wiki/Before_Common_Era" class="mw-redirect" title="Before Common Era">BCE</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Turrialba_(district)" title="Turrialba (district)">Turrialba Valley</a>. The presence of <a href="/wiki/Clovis_culture" title="Clovis culture">Clovis culture</a> type spearheads and arrows from <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a> opens the possibility that, in this area, two different cultures coexisted.<sup id="cite_ref-precolumbian1_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-precolumbian1-17">[17]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Agriculture became evident in the populations that lived in Costa Rica about 5,000 years ago. They mainly grew <a href="/wiki/Tubers" class="mw-redirect" title="Tubers">tubers</a> and roots. For the first and second millennia BCE there were already settled farming communities. These were small and scattered, although the timing of the transition from <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunting</a> and gathering to agriculture as the main livelihood in the territory is still unknown.<sup id="cite_ref-precolumbian2_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-precolumbian2-18">[18]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The earliest use of <a href="/wiki/Pottery" title="Pottery">pottery</a> appears around 2,000 to 3,000 BCE. Shards of pots, cylindrical vases, platters, gourds and other forms of vases decorated with grooves, prints, and some modelled after animals have been found.<sup id="cite_ref-precolumban3_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-precolumban3-19">[19]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The impact of indigenous peoples on modern Costa Rican culture has been relatively small compared to other nations, since the country lacked a strong native civilization to begin with. Most of the native population was absorbed into the Spanish-speaking <a href="/wiki/Spanish_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="Spanish colonization of the Americas">colonial society</a> through inter-marriage, except for some small remnants, the most significant of which are the <a href="/wiki/Bribri_people" title="Bribri people">Bribri</a> and <a href="/wiki/Boruca_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Boruca people">Boruca</a> tribes who still inhabit the mountains of the <a href="/wiki/Cordillera_de_Talamanca" title="Cordillera de Talamanca">Cordillera de Talamanca</a>, in the southeastern part of Costa Rica, near the frontier with <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">Panama</a>. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spanish_colonization">Spanish colonization</span></h3> 
      <p>The name <i lang="es" title="Spanish-language text">la costa rica</i>, meaning "rich coast" in the Spanish language, was in some accounts first applied by <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">Christopher Columbus</a>, who sailed to the eastern shores of Costa Rica during his final voyage in 1502,<sup id="cite_ref-embassy_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-embassy-20">[20]</a></sup> and reported vast quantities of gold jewelry worn by natives.<sup id="cite_ref-lp_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lp-21">[21]</a></sup> The name may also have come from conquistador <a href="/wiki/Gil_Gonz%C3%A1lez_D%C3%A1vila" title="Gil González Dávila">Gil González Dávila</a>, who landed on the west coast in 1522, encountered natives, and obtained some of their gold, sometimes by violent theft and sometimes as gifts from local leaders.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Iglesia_Inmaculada_Concepcion_Ujarras.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Iglesia_Inmaculada_Concepcion_Ujarras.jpg/220px-Iglesia_Inmaculada_Concepcion_Ujarras.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Iglesia_Inmaculada_Concepcion_Ujarras.jpg/330px-Iglesia_Inmaculada_Concepcion_Ujarras.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Iglesia_Inmaculada_Concepcion_Ujarras.jpg/440px-Iglesia_Inmaculada_Concepcion_Ujarras.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4752" data-file-height="3168"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Iglesia_Inmaculada_Concepcion_Ujarras.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <span lang="es" style="font-style: normal;" title="Spanish-language text"><a href="/wiki/Ujarr%C3%A1s" title="Ujarrás">Ujarrás</a></span> historical site in the Orosí Valley, <a href="/wiki/Cartago_province" class="mw-redirect" title="Cartago province">Cartago province</a>. The church was built between 1686 and 1693.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>During most of the colonial period, Costa Rica was the southernmost province of the <a href="/wiki/Captaincy_General_of_Guatemala" title="Captaincy General of Guatemala">Captaincy General of Guatemala</a>, nominally part of the <a href="/wiki/New_Spain" title="New Spain">Viceroyalty of New Spain</a>. In practice, the captaincy general was a largely autonomous entity within the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">Spanish Empire</a>. Costa Rica's distance from the capital of the captaincy in <a href="/wiki/Guatemala" title="Guatemala">Guatemala</a>, its legal prohibition under Spanish law from trade with its southern neighbor Panama, then part of the <a href="/wiki/Viceroyalty_of_New_Granada" title="Viceroyalty of New Granada">Viceroyalty of New Granada</a> (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a>), and lack of resources such as gold and silver, made Costa Rica into a poor, isolated, and sparsely-inhabited region within the Spanish Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Costa Rica was described as "the poorest and most miserable Spanish colony in all America" by a Spanish governor in 1719.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Another important factor behind Costa Rica's poverty was the lack of a significant indigenous population available for <i lang="es" title="Spanish-language text"><a href="/wiki/Encomienda" title="Encomienda">encomienda</a></i> (forced labor), which meant most of the Costa Rican settlers had to work on their own land, preventing the establishment of large <i lang="es" title="Spanish-language text"><a href="/wiki/Hacienda" title="Hacienda">haciendas</a></i> (plantations). For all these reasons, Costa Rica was, by and large, unappreciated and overlooked by the <a href="/wiki/Habsburg_Spain" title="Habsburg Spain">Spanish Crown</a> and left to develop on its own. The circumstances during this period are believed to have led to many of the idiosyncrasies for which Costa Rica has become known, while concomitantly setting the stage for Costa Rica's development as a more egalitarian society than the rest of its neighbors. Costa Rica became a "rural democracy" with no oppressed <a href="/wiki/Mestizo" title="Mestizo">mestizo</a> or indigenous class. It was not long before Spanish settlers turned to the hills, where they found rich volcanic soil and a milder climate than that of the lowlands.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Independence">Independence</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Free_State_of_Costa_Rica" title="Free State of Costa Rica">Free State of Costa Rica</a> and <a href="/wiki/First_Costa_Rican_Republic" title="First Costa Rican Republic">First Costa Rican Republic</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Like the rest of <a href="/wiki/Central_America" title="Central America">Central America</a>, Costa Rica never fought for independence from Spain. On 15 September 1821, after the final Spanish defeat in the <a href="/wiki/Mexican_War_of_Independence" title="Mexican War of Independence">Mexican War of Independence</a> (1810–21), the authorities in Guatemala declared the independence of all of Central America. That date is still celebrated as Independence Day in Costa Rica<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> even though, technically, under the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Constitution_of_1812" title="Spanish Constitution of 1812">Spanish Constitution of 1812</a> that had been readopted in 1820, <a href="/wiki/Nicaragua" title="Nicaragua">Nicaragua</a> and Costa Rica had become an autonomous province with its capital in <a href="/wiki/Le%C3%B3n,_Nicaragua" title="León, Nicaragua">León</a>. </p>
      <p>Upon independence, Costa Rican authorities faced the issue of officially deciding the future of the country. Two bands formed, the Imperialists, defended by <a href="/wiki/Cartago,_Costa_Rica" title="Cartago, Costa Rica">Cartago</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heredia_Province" title="Heredia Province">Heredia cities</a> which were in favor of joining the <a href="/wiki/First_Mexican_Empire" title="First Mexican Empire">Mexican Empire</a>, and the Republicans, represented by the cities of <a href="/wiki/San_Jos%C3%A9,_Costa_Rica" title="San José, Costa Rica">San José</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alajuela" title="Alajuela">Alajuela</a> who defended full independence. Because of the lack of agreement on these two possible outcomes, the first civil war of Costa Rica occurred. The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ochomogo" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Ochomogo">Battle of Ochomogo</a> took place on the Hill of Ochomogo, located in the <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rican_Central_Valley" title="Costa Rican Central Valley">Central Valley</a> in 1823. The conflict was won by the Republicans and, as a consequence, the city of <a href="/wiki/Cartago,_Costa_Rica" title="Cartago, Costa Rica">Cartago</a> lost its status as the capital, which moved to <a href="/wiki/San_Jos%C3%A9,_Costa_Rica" title="San José, Costa Rica">San José</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:First_postal_stamp_CR_4_Reales_1863.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/First_postal_stamp_CR_4_Reales_1863.jpg/220px-First_postal_stamp_CR_4_Reales_1863.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="261" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/First_postal_stamp_CR_4_Reales_1863.jpg/330px-First_postal_stamp_CR_4_Reales_1863.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/First_postal_stamp_CR_4_Reales_1863.jpg/440px-First_postal_stamp_CR_4_Reales_1863.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="949"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:First_postal_stamp_CR_4_Reales_1863.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The 1849 <a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms_of_Costa_Rica" title="Coat of arms of Costa Rica">national coat of arms</a> was featured in the first postal stamp issued in 1862.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In 1838, long after the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Republic_of_Central_America" title="Federal Republic of Central America">Federal Republic of Central America</a> ceased to function in practice, Costa Rica formally withdrew and proclaimed itself sovereign. The considerable distance and poor communication routes between <a href="/wiki/Guatemala_City" title="Guatemala City">Guatemala City</a> and the Central Plateau, where most of the Costa Rican population lived then and still lives now, meant the local population had little allegiance to the federal government in Guatemala. From colonial times to now, Costa Rica's reluctance to become economically tied with the rest of Central America has been a major obstacle to efforts for greater regional integration.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Until 1849, when it became part of <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">Panama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chiriqu%C3%AD_province" class="mw-redirect" title="Chiriquí province">Chiriquí</a> was part of Costa Rica. Costa Rican pride was assuaged for the loss of this eastern (or southern) territory with the acquisition of <a href="/wiki/Guanacaste_Province" title="Guanacaste Province">Guanacaste</a>, in the north. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_growth_in_the_19th_century">Economic growth in the 19th century</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Liberal_State" title="Liberal State">Liberal State</a>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Coffee" title="Coffee">Coffee</a> was first planted in Costa Rica in 1808,<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> and by the 1820s, it surpassed <a href="/wiki/Tobacco" title="Tobacco">tobacco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugar</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cacao_bean" class="mw-redirect" title="Cacao bean">cacao</a> as a primary <a href="/wiki/Export" title="Export">export</a>. Coffee production remained Costa Rica's principal source of wealth well into the 20th century, creating a wealthy class of growers, the so-called Coffee Barons.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> The revenue helped to modernize the country.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-cafeimports.com_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cafeimports.com-34">[34]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Most of the coffee exported was grown around the main centers of population in the Central Plateau and then transported by <a href="/wiki/Bullock_cart" title="Bullock cart">oxcart</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific</a> port of <a href="/wiki/Puntarenas" title="Puntarenas">Puntarenas</a> after the main road was built in 1846.<sup id="cite_ref-cafeimports.com_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cafeimports.com-34">[34]</a></sup> By the mid-1850s the main market for coffee was Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> It soon became a high priority to develop an effective transportation route from the Central Plateau to the Atlantic Ocean. For this purpose, in the 1870s, the Costa Rican government contracted with U.S. businessman <a href="/wiki/Minor_C._Keith" class="mw-redirect" title="Minor C. Keith">Minor C. Keith</a> to build a railroad from San José to the <a href="/wiki/Western_Caribbean_Zone" class="mw-redirect" title="Western Caribbean Zone">Caribbean</a> port of <a href="/wiki/Lim%C3%B3n" title="Limón">Limón</a>. Despite enormous difficulties with construction, disease, and financing, the railroad was completed in 1890.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Most Afro-Costa Ricans descend from <a href="/wiki/Jamaica" title="Jamaica">Jamaican</a> immigrants who worked in the construction of that railway and now make up about 3% of Costa Rica's population.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> U.S. convicts, Italians and Chinese immigrants also participated in the construction project. In exchange for completing the railroad, the Costa Rican government granted Keith large tracts of land and a lease on the train route, which he used to produce bananas and export them to the United States. As a result, bananas came to rival coffee as the principal Costa Rican export, while foreign-owned corporations (including the <a href="/wiki/United_Fruit_Company" title="United Fruit Company">United Fruit Company</a> later) began to hold a major role in the national economy and eventually became a symbol of the exploitative export economy.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> The major labor dispute between the peasants and the United Fruit Company (The Great Banana Strike) was a major event in the country's history and was an important step that would eventually lead to the formation of effective <a href="/wiki/Trade_unions_in_Costa_Rica" title="Trade unions in Costa Rica">trade unions in Costa Rica</a>, as the company was required to sign a collective agreement with its workers in 1938.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century">20th century</span></h4> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Reform_State" title="Reform State">Reform State</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Historically, Costa Rica has generally enjoyed greater peace and more consistent political stability than many of its fellow Latin American nations. Since the late 19th century, however, Costa Rica has experienced two significant periods of violence. In 1917–19, General <a href="/wiki/Federico_Tinoco_Granados" title="Federico Tinoco Granados">Federico Tinoco Granados</a> ruled as a military dictator until he was overthrown and forced into exile. The unpopularity of <a href="/wiki/Dictatorship_of_the_Tinoco_Brothers" title="Dictatorship of the Tinoco Brothers">Tinoco's regime</a> led, after he was overthrown, to a considerable decline in the size, wealth, and political influence of the Costa Rican military. In 1948, <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Figueres_Ferrer" title="José Figueres Ferrer">José Figueres Ferrer</a> led an <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rican_Civil_War" title="Costa Rican Civil War">armed uprising</a> in the wake of a disputed presidential election between <a href="/wiki/Rafael_%C3%81ngel_Calder%C3%B3n_Guardia" title="Rafael Ángel Calderón Guardia">Rafael Ángel Calderón Guardia</a> (who had been president between 1940 and 1944) and <a href="/wiki/Otilio_Ulate_Blanco" title="Otilio Ulate Blanco">Otilio Ulate Blanco</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> With more than 2,000 dead, the resulting 44-day <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rican_Civil_War" title="Costa Rican Civil War">Costa Rican Civil War</a> was the bloodiest event in Costa Rica during the 20th century. </p>
      <p>The victorious rebels formed a government junta that <a href="/wiki/Military_of_Costa_Rica" class="mw-redirect" title="Military of Costa Rica">abolished the military</a> altogether, and oversaw the drafting of a new constitution by a democratically elected assembly.<sup id="cite_ref-nytimes-military_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes-military-42">[42]</a></sup> Having enacted these reforms, the junta transferred power to Ulate on 8 November 1949. After the <i>coup d'état</i>, Figueres became a national hero, winning the country's first democratic election under the new constitution <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rican_general_election,_1953" class="mw-redirect" title="Costa Rican general election, 1953">in 1953</a>. Since then, Costa Rica has held 14 presidential elections, the latest <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rican_general_election,_2018" class="mw-redirect" title="Costa Rican general election, 2018">in 2018</a>. With uninterrupted democracy dating back to at least 1948, the country is the region's most stable.<sup id="cite_ref-peace_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-peace-43">[43]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pacifism_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pacifism-44">[44]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Costa_Rica" title="Geography of Costa Rica">Geography of Costa Rica</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Koppen-Geiger_Map_CRI_present.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Koppen-Geiger_Map_CRI_present.svg/290px-Koppen-Geiger_Map_CRI_present.svg.png" decoding="async" width="290" height="192" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Koppen-Geiger_Map_CRI_present.svg/435px-Koppen-Geiger_Map_CRI_present.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Koppen-Geiger_Map_CRI_present.svg/580px-Koppen-Geiger_Map_CRI_present.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1281" data-file-height="850"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Koppen-Geiger_Map_CRI_present.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Costa Rica map of Köppen climate classification
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Costa Rica borders the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Sea" title="Caribbean Sea">Caribbean Sea</a> to the east, and the Pacific Ocean to the west. Costa Rica also borders <a href="/wiki/Nicaragua" title="Nicaragua">Nicaragua</a> to the north and <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">Panama</a> to the south. </p>
      <p>The highest point in the country is <a href="/wiki/Cerro_Chirrip%C3%B3" class="mw-redirect" title="Cerro Chirripó">Cerro Chirripó</a>, at 3,819 metres (12,530&nbsp;ft). The highest volcano in the country is the <a href="/wiki/Iraz%C3%BA_Volcano" title="Irazú Volcano">Irazú Volcano</a> (3,431&nbsp;m or 11,257&nbsp;ft) and the largest lake is <a href="/wiki/Lake_Arenal" title="Lake Arenal">Lake Arenal</a>. There are 14 known volcanoes in Costa Rica, and six of them have been active in the last 75 years.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span></h3> 
      <p>Costa Rica experiences a <a href="/wiki/Tropical_climate" title="Tropical climate">tropical climate</a> year round. There are two seasons. The "summer" or dry season is December to April, and "winter" or rainy season is May to November. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flora_and_fauna">Flora and fauna</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Red_eyed_tree_frog_edit2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Red_eyed_tree_frog_edit2.jpg/220px-Red_eyed_tree_frog_edit2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Red_eyed_tree_frog_edit2.jpg/330px-Red_eyed_tree_frog_edit2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Red_eyed_tree_frog_edit2.jpg/440px-Red_eyed_tree_frog_edit2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2293" data-file-height="1720"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Red_eyed_tree_frog_edit2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Red-eyed tree frog (<i><a href="/wiki/Agalychnis_callidryas" title="Agalychnis callidryas">Agalychnis callidryas</a></i>)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>There is a rich variety of plants and <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_of_Costa_Rica" title="Wildlife of Costa Rica">Costa Rican wildlife</a>. </p>
      <p>One national park, the <a href="/wiki/Corcovado_National_Park" title="Corcovado National Park">Corcovado National Park</a>, is internationally renowned among ecologists for its biodiversity (including <a href="/wiki/Big_cats" class="mw-redirect" title="Big cats">big cats</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tapirs" class="mw-redirect" title="Tapirs">tapirs</a>) and is where visitors can expect to see an abundance of wildlife.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Corcovado is the one park in Costa Rica where <a href="/wiki/List_of_Costa_Rican_monkey_species" title="List of Costa Rican monkey species">all four Costa Rican monkey species</a> can be found.<sup id="cite_ref-watching_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-watching-48">[48]</a></sup> These include the <a href="/wiki/White-headed_capuchin" class="mw-redirect" title="White-headed capuchin">white-headed capuchin</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Mantled_howler" title="Mantled howler">mantled howler</a>, the endangered <a href="/wiki/Geoffroy%27s_spider_monkey" title="Geoffroy's spider monkey">Geoffroy's spider monkey</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-watching_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-watching-48">[48]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Central_American_squirrel_monkey" title="Central American squirrel monkey">Central American squirrel monkey</a>, found only on the Pacific coast of Costa Rica and a small part of <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">Panama</a>, and considered endangered until 2008, when its status was upgraded to vulnerable. <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">Deforestation</a>, illegal pet-trading, and hunting are the main reasons for its threatened status.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Costa Rica is the first tropical country to have stopped and reversed deforestation; it has successfully restored its forestry and developed an ecosystem service to teach biologists and ecologists about its environmental protection measures.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> The country had a 2018 <a href="/wiki/Forest_Landscape_Integrity_Index" title="Forest Landscape Integrity Index">Forest Landscape Integrity Index</a> mean score of 4.65/10, ranking it 118th globally out of 172 countries.<sup id="cite_ref-FLII-Supplementary_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FLII-Supplementary-52">[52]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span></h2> 
      <table class="box-Update plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Update" role="presentation">
       <tbody>
        <tr>
         <td class="mbox-image">
          <div style="width:52px">
           <img alt="Ambox current red Asia Australia.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Ambox_current_red_Asia_Australia.svg/42px-Ambox_current_red_Asia_Australia.svg.png" decoding="async" width="42" height="34" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Ambox_current_red_Asia_Australia.svg/63px-Ambox_current_red_Asia_Australia.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Ambox_current_red_Asia_Australia.svg/84px-Ambox_current_red_Asia_Australia.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="360" data-file-height="290">
          </div></td>
         <td class="mbox-text">
          <div class="mbox-text-span">
           This section needs to be <b>updated</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please update this article to reflect recent events or newly available information.</span> <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">September 2019</span>)</i></small>
          </div></td>
        </tr>
       </tbody>
      </table> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Intel_Costa_12_2007_SJO_105b.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Intel_Costa_12_2007_SJO_105b.jpg/220px-Intel_Costa_12_2007_SJO_105b.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Intel_Costa_12_2007_SJO_105b.jpg/330px-Intel_Costa_12_2007_SJO_105b.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Intel_Costa_12_2007_SJO_105b.jpg/440px-Intel_Costa_12_2007_SJO_105b.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1120" data-file-height="747"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Intel_Costa_12_2007_SJO_105b.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>An <a href="/wiki/Intel" title="Intel">Intel</a> microprocessor facility in Costa Rica that was, at one time, responsible for 20% of Costa Rican exports and 5% of the country's GDP.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Costa_Rica" title="Economy of Costa Rica">Economy of Costa Rica</a>
      </div> 
      <p>The country has been considered economically stable with moderate inflation, estimated at 2.6% in 2017,<sup id="cite_ref-gfmag.com_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gfmag.com-53">[53]</a></sup> and moderately high growth in GDP, which increased from US$41.3 billion in 2011 to US$52.6 billion in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-focus-economics.com_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-focus-economics.com-54">[54]</a></sup> The estimated GDP for 2018 is US$59.0 billion and the estimated GDP per capita (purchasing power parity) is Intl$17,559.1.<sup id="cite_ref-gfmag.com_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gfmag.com-53">[53]</a></sup> The growing <a href="/wiki/Debt" title="Debt">debt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Budget_deficit" class="mw-redirect" title="Budget deficit">budget deficit</a> are the country's primary concerns.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-55">[55]</a></sup> A 2017 study by the Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development warned that reducing the foreign debt must be a very high priority for the government. Other fiscal reforms were also recommended to moderate the budget deficit.<sup id="cite_ref-news.co.cr_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.co.cr-56">[56]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Many foreign companies (manufacturing and services) operate in Costa Rica's Free Trade Zones (FTZ) where they benefit from investment and tax incentives.<sup id="cite_ref-cinde.org_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cinde.org-12">[12]</a></sup> Well over half of that type of investment has come from the U.S.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> According to the government, the zones supported over 82,000 direct jobs and 43,000 indirect jobs in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-58">[58]</a></sup> Companies with facilities in the America Free Zone in Heredia, for example, include Intel, Dell, HP, Bayer, Bosch, DHL, IBM and Okay Industries.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Of the GDP, 5.5% is generated by agriculture, 18.6% by industry and 75.9% by services. (2016)<sup id="cite_ref-gfmag.com_53-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gfmag.com-53">[53]</a></sup> Agriculture employs 12.9% of the labor force, industry 18.57%, services 69.02% (2016)<sup id="cite_ref-statista.com_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-statista.com-61">[61]</a></sup> For the region, its unemployment level is moderately high (8.2% in 2016, according to the IMF).<sup id="cite_ref-gfmag.com_53-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gfmag.com-53">[53]</a></sup> Although 20.5% of the population lives below the poverty line (2017),<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Costa Rica has one of the highest standards of living in Central America.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>High quality health care is provided by the government at low cost to the users.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Housing is also very affordable. Costa Rica is recognized in Latin America for the quality of its educational system. Because of its educational system, Costa Rica has one of the highest literacy rates in Latin America, 97%.<sup id="cite_ref-2016.export.gov_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2016.export.gov-65">[65]</a></sup> General Basic Education is mandatory and provided without cost to the user.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> A US government report confirms that the country has "historically placed a high priority on education and the creation of a skilled work force" but notes that the high school drop-out rate is increasing. As well, Costa Rica would benefit from more courses in languages such as English, Portuguese, Mandarin and French and also in Science, Technology, Engineering and Math (STEM).<sup id="cite_ref-2016.export.gov_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2016.export.gov-65">[65]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trade_and_foreign_investment">Trade and foreign investment</span></h3> 
      <p>Costa Rica has free trade agreements with many countries, including the US. There are no significant trade barriers that would affect imports and the country has been lowering its tariffs in accordance with other Central American countries.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> The country's Free Trade Zones provide incentives for manufacturing and service industries to operate in Costa Rica. In 2015, the zones supported over 82 thousand direct jobs and 43 thousand indirect jobs in 2015 and average wages in the FTZ were 1.8 times greater than the average for private enterprise work in the rest of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-58">[58]</a></sup> In 2016, <a href="/wiki/Amazon.com" class="mw-redirect" title="Amazon.com">Amazon.com</a> for example, had some 3,500 employees in Costa Rica and planned to increase that by 1,500 in 2017, making it an important employer.<sup id="cite_ref-seattletimes.com_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-seattletimes.com-11">[11]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The central location provides access to American markets and direct ocean access to Europe and Asia. The most important exports in 2015 (in order of dollar value) were medical instruments, bananas, tropical fruits, integrated circuits and orthopedic appliances.<sup id="cite_ref-atlas.media.mit.edu_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atlas.media.mit.edu-68">[68]</a></sup> Total imports in that year were US$15 billion. The most significant products imported in 2015 (in order of dollar value) were refined petroleum, automobiles, packaged medications, broadcasting equipment and computers. The total exports were US$12.6 billion for a trade deficit of US$2.39 billion in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-atlas.media.mit.edu_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-atlas.media.mit.edu-68">[68]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:DirkvdM_orosi_valley_bird.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/DirkvdM_orosi_valley_bird.jpg/220px-DirkvdM_orosi_valley_bird.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/DirkvdM_orosi_valley_bird.jpg/330px-DirkvdM_orosi_valley_bird.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/DirkvdM_orosi_valley_bird.jpg/440px-DirkvdM_orosi_valley_bird.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1136" data-file-height="852"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:DirkvdM_orosi_valley_bird.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A <a href="/wiki/Coffee_production_in_Costa_Rica" title="Coffee production in Costa Rica">coffee plantation</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Oros%C3%AD" class="mw-redirect" title="Orosí">Orosí Valley</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Pharmaceuticals, financial outsourcing, software development, and <a href="/wiki/Ecotourism" title="Ecotourism">ecotourism</a> have become the prime industries in Costa Rica's economy. High levels of education among its residents make the country an attractive investing location. Since 1999, tourism earns more foreign exchange than the combined exports of the country's three main <a href="/wiki/Cash_crops" class="mw-redirect" title="Cash crops">cash crops</a>: bananas and pineapples especially,<sup id="cite_ref-qcostarica.com_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-qcostarica.com-69">[69]</a></sup> but also other crops, including coffee.<sup id="cite_ref-ICT2006_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ICT2006-70">[70]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Coffee_production_in_Costa_Rica" title="Coffee production in Costa Rica">Coffee production</a> played a key role in Costa Rica's history and in 2006, was the third cash crop export.<sup id="cite_ref-ICT2006_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ICT2006-70">[70]</a></sup> As a small country, Costa Rica now provides under 1% of the world's coffee production.<sup id="cite_ref-cafeimports.com_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cafeimports.com-34">[34]</a></sup> In 2015, the value of coffee exports was US$305.9 million, a small part of the total agricultural exports of US$2.7 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-qcostarica.com_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-qcostarica.com-69">[69]</a></sup> Coffee production increased by 13.7% percent in 2015–16, declined by 17.5% in 2016–17, but was expected to increase by about 15% in the subsequent year.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Costa Rica has developed a system of <a href="/wiki/Payment_for_ecosystem_services" title="Payment for ecosystem services">payments for environmental services</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ODI1_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODI1-72">[72]</a></sup> Similarly, Costa Rica has a tax on water pollution to penalize businesses and homeowners that dump sewage, agricultural chemicals, and other pollutants into waterways.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> In May 2007, the Costa Rican government announced its intentions to become 100% <a href="/wiki/Carbon_neutral" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon neutral">carbon neutral</a> by 2021.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> By 2015, 93 percent of the country's electricity came from <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_in_Costa_Rica" title="Renewable energy in Costa Rica">renewable sources</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> In 2019, the country produced 99.62% of its electricity from renewable sources and ran completely on renewable sources for 300 continuous days.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1996, the Forest Law was enacted to provide direct financial incentives to landowners for the provision of environmental services.<sup id="cite_ref-ODI1_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODI1-72">[72]</a></sup> This helped reorient the forestry sector away from commercial timber production and the resulting <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a>, and helped create awareness of the services it provides for the economy and society (i.e., <a href="/wiki/Carbon_fixation" title="Carbon fixation">carbon fixation</a>, hydrological services such as producing fresh drinking water, biodiversity protection, and provision of scenic beauty).<sup id="cite_ref-ODI1_72-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODI1-72">[72]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>A 2016 report by the U.S. government report identifies other challenges facing Costa Rica as it works to expand its economy by working with companies from the US (and probably from other countries).<sup id="cite_ref-2016.export.gov_65-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2016.export.gov-65">[65]</a></sup> The major concerns identified were as follows: </p> 
      <ul>
       <li>The ports, roads, railways and water delivery systems would benefit from major upgrading, a concern voiced by other reports too.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Attempts by China to invest in upgrading such aspects were "stalled by bureaucratic and legal concerns".</li> 
       <li>The bureaucracy is "often slow and cumbersome".</li>
      </ul> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tourism">Tourism</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tourism_in_Costa_Rica" title="Tourism in Costa Rica">Tourism in Costa Rica</a>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_airports_in_Costa_Rica" title="List of airports in Costa Rica">List of airports in Costa Rica</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Poas_crater.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Poas_crater.jpg/220px-Poas_crater.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Poas_crater.jpg/330px-Poas_crater.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Poas_crater.jpg/440px-Poas_crater.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Poas_crater.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Po%C3%A1s_Volcano_National_Park" title="Poás Volcano National Park">Poás Volcano Crater</a> is one of the country's main <a href="/wiki/Tourism_in_Costa_Rica" title="Tourism in Costa Rica">tourist attractions</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Costa Rica is the most-visited nation in the Central American region,<sup id="cite_ref-Statista_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Statista-78">[78]</a></sup> with 2.9 million foreign visitors in 2016, up 10% from 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-Central_America_Data_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Central_America_Data-79">[79]</a></sup> In 2015, the tourism sector was responsible for 5.8% of the country's GDP, or $3.4 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> In 2016, the highest number of tourists came from the United States, with 1,000,000 visitors, followed by Europe with 434,884 arrivals.<sup id="cite_ref-El_Financiero_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-El_Financiero-81">[81]</a></sup> According to Costa Rica Vacations, once tourists arrive in the country, 22% go to <a href="/wiki/Tamarindo,_Costa_Rica" title="Tamarindo, Costa Rica">Tamarindo</a>, 18% go to <a href="/wiki/Arenal,_Costa_Rica" class="mw-redirect" title="Arenal, Costa Rica">Arenal</a>, 17% pass through <a href="/wiki/Liberia,_Costa_Rica" title="Liberia, Costa Rica">Liberia</a> (where the <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Oduber_Quir%C3%B3s_International_Airport" title="Daniel Oduber Quirós International Airport">Daniel Oduber Quirós International Airport</a> is located), 16% go to <a href="/wiki/San_Jos%C3%A9,_Costa_Rica" title="San José, Costa Rica">San José</a>, the country's capital (passing through <a href="/wiki/Juan_Santamar%C3%ADa_International_Airport" title="Juan Santamaría International Airport">Juan Santamaría International Airport</a>), while 18% choose Manuel Antonio and 7% Monteverde.<sup id="cite_ref-Costa_RIca_Vacations_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Costa_RIca_Vacations-82">[82]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>By 2004, tourism was generating more revenue and <a href="/wiki/Foreign_exchange_reserves" title="Foreign exchange reserves">foreign exchange</a> than bananas and coffee combined.<sup id="cite_ref-ICT2006_70-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ICT2006-70">[70]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> In 2016, the World Travel &amp; Tourism Council's estimates indicated a direct contribution to the GDP of 5.1% and 110,000 direct jobs in Costa Rica; the total number of jobs indirectly supported by tourism was 271,000.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>A pioneer of <a href="/wiki/Ecotourism" title="Ecotourism">ecotourism</a>, Costa Rica draws many tourists to its extensive series of national parks and other protected areas.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> The trail <a href="/wiki/Camino_de_Costa_Rica" title="Camino de Costa Rica">Camino de Costa Rica</a> supports this by allowing travelers to walk across the country from the Atlantic to the Pacific coast. In the 2011 <a href="/wiki/Travel_and_Tourism_Competitiveness_Report" title="Travel and Tourism Competitiveness Report">Travel and Tourism Competitiveness Index</a>, Costa Rica ranked 44th in the world and second among Latin American countries after Mexico in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-TTCI2011_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TTCI2011-86">[86]</a></sup> By the time of the 2017 report, the country had reached 38th place, slightly behind Panama.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> The Ethical Traveler group's ten countries on their 2017 list of The World's Ten Best Ethical Destinations includes Costa Rica. The country scored highest in environmental protection among the winners.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Costa Rica began reversing <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a> in the 1990s, and they are moving towards using only <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government_and_politics">Government and politics</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Costa_Rica" title="Politics of Costa Rica">Politics of Costa Rica</a>
      </div> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Administrative_divisions">Administrative divisions</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Provinces_Costa_Rica.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Provinces_Costa_Rica.png/220px-Provinces_Costa_Rica.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="202" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Provinces_Costa_Rica.png/330px-Provinces_Costa_Rica.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/00/Provinces_Costa_Rica.png 2x" data-file-width="345" data-file-height="317"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Provinces_Costa_Rica.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Provinces: 1 <a href="/wiki/Alajuela_Province" title="Alajuela Province">Alajuela</a>, 2 <a href="/wiki/Cartago_Province" title="Cartago Province">Cartago</a>, 3 <a href="/wiki/Guanacaste_Province" title="Guanacaste Province">Guanacaste</a>, 4 <a href="/wiki/Heredia_Province" title="Heredia Province">Heredia</a>, 5 <a href="/wiki/Lim%C3%B3n_Province" title="Limón Province">Limón</a>, 6 <a href="/wiki/Puntarenas_Province" title="Puntarenas Province">Puntarenas</a>, 7 <a href="/wiki/San_Jos%C3%A9_Province" title="San José Province">San José</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Administrative_divisions_of_Costa_Rica" title="Administrative divisions of Costa Rica">Administrative divisions of Costa Rica</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Costa Rica is composed of seven provinces, which in turn are divided into 82 cantons (Spanish: <i lang="es">cantón</i>, plural <i lang="es" title="Spanish-language text">cantones</i>), each of which is directed by a mayor. Mayors are chosen democratically every four years by each canton. There are no provincial legislatures. The cantons are further divided into 488 districts (<i lang="es" title="Spanish-language text">distritos</i>). </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_relations">Foreign relations</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Costa_Rica" title="Foreign relations of Costa Rica">Foreign relations of Costa Rica</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Localisation_de_l%27ile_de_Clipperton.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Localisation_de_l%27ile_de_Clipperton.png/440px-Localisation_de_l%27ile_de_Clipperton.png" decoding="async" width="440" height="310" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Localisation_de_l%27ile_de_Clipperton.png/660px-Localisation_de_l%27ile_de_Clipperton.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/60/Localisation_de_l%27ile_de_Clipperton.png 2x" data-file-width="783" data-file-height="551"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Localisation_de_l%27ile_de_Clipperton.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The extent of Costa Rica's western <a href="/wiki/EEZ" class="mw-redirect" title="EEZ">EEZ</a> in the Pacific
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Barack_Obama_with_Costa_Rican_children.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Barack_Obama_with_Costa_Rican_children.jpg/220px-Barack_Obama_with_Costa_Rican_children.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Barack_Obama_with_Costa_Rican_children.jpg/330px-Barack_Obama_with_Costa_Rican_children.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Barack_Obama_with_Costa_Rican_children.jpg/440px-Barack_Obama_with_Costa_Rican_children.jpg 2x" data-file-width="654" data-file-height="436"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Barack_Obama_with_Costa_Rican_children.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> and <a href="/wiki/Laura_Chinchilla" title="Laura Chinchilla">Laura Chinchilla</a> with Costa Rican children in <a href="/wiki/San_Jos%C3%A9,_Costa_Rica" title="San José, Costa Rica">San José</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Costa Rica is an active member of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Organization_of_American_States" title="Organization of American States">Organization of American States</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Inter-American_Court_of_Human_Rights" title="Inter-American Court of Human Rights">Inter-American Court of Human Rights</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_University_of_Peace" class="mw-redirect" title="United Nations University of Peace">United Nations University of Peace</a> are based in Costa Rica. It is also a member of many other international organizations related to <a href="/wiki/Human_rights" title="Human rights">human rights</a> and democracy, such as the <a href="/wiki/Community_of_Democracies" title="Community of Democracies">Community of Democracies</a>. A main foreign policy objective of Costa Rica is to foster human rights and sustainable development as a way to secure stability and growth.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Costa Rica is a member of the <a href="/wiki/International_Criminal_Court" title="International Criminal Court">International Criminal Court</a>, without a <a href="/wiki/Bilateral_Immunity_Agreement" class="mw-redirect" title="Bilateral Immunity Agreement">Bilateral Immunity Agreement</a> of protection for the <a href="/wiki/United_States_military" class="mw-redirect" title="United States military">United States military</a> (as covered under <a href="/wiki/Rome_Statute_of_the_International_Criminal_Court" title="Rome Statute of the International Criminal Court">Article 98</a>). Costa Rica is an observer of the <a href="/wiki/Organisation_internationale_de_la_Francophonie" title="Organisation internationale de la Francophonie">Organisation internationale de la Francophonie</a>. </p>
      <p>On 10 September 1961, some months after <a href="/wiki/Fidel_Castro" title="Fidel Castro">Fidel Castro</a> declared <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a> a socialist state, Costa Rican President <a href="/wiki/Mario_Echandi" class="mw-redirect" title="Mario Echandi">Mario Echandi</a> ended diplomatic relations with Cuba through <i>Executive Decree Number 2</i>. This freeze lasted 47 years until President <a href="/wiki/%C3%93scar_Arias" title="Óscar Arias">Óscar Arias Sánchez</a> re-established normal relations on 18 March 2009, saying, "If we have been able to turn the page with regimes as profoundly different to our reality as occurred with the USSR or, more recently, with the Republic of China, how would we not do it with a country that is geographically and culturally much nearer to Costa Rica?" Arias announced that both countries would exchange ambassadors.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Costa Rica has a long-term disagreement with <a href="/wiki/Nicaragua" title="Nicaragua">Nicaragua</a> over the <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rica_%E2%80%93_Nicaragua_San_Juan_River_border_dispute" class="mw-redirect" title="Costa Rica – Nicaragua San Juan River border dispute">San Juan River</a>, which defines the border between the two countries, and Costa Rica's <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_navigation" title="Freedom of navigation">rights of navigation</a> on the river.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> In 2010, there was also a <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rica_%E2%80%93_Nicaragua_San_Juan_River_border_dispute#2010_Isla_Calero_dispute" class="mw-redirect" title="Costa Rica – Nicaragua San Juan River border dispute">dispute around Isla Calero</a>, and the impact of Nicaraguan <a href="/wiki/Dredging" title="Dredging">dredging</a> of the river in that area.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On 14 July 2009, the <a href="/wiki/International_Court_of_Justice" title="International Court of Justice">International Court of Justice</a> in the Hague upheld Costa Rica's navigation rights for commercial purposes to <a href="/wiki/Artisanal_fishing" title="Artisanal fishing">subsistence fishing</a> on their side of the river. An 1858 treaty extended navigation rights to Costa Rica, but Nicaragua denied passenger travel and fishing were part of the deal; the court ruled Costa Ricans on the river were not required to have Nicaraguan tourist cards or visas as Nicaragua argued, but, in a nod to the Nicaraguans, ruled that Costa Rican boats and passengers must stop at the first and last Nicaraguan port along their route. They must also have an identity document or passport. Nicaragua can also impose timetables on Costa Rican traffic. Nicaragua may require Costa Rican boats to display the <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Nicaragua" title="Flag of Nicaragua">flag of Nicaragua</a>, but may not charge them for departure clearance from its ports. These were all specific items of contention brought to the court in the 2005 filing.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On 1 June 2007, Costa Rica broke diplomatic ties with <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>, switching recognition to the People's Republic of China. Costa Rica was the first of the Central American nations to do so. President <a href="/wiki/%C3%93scar_Arias_S%C3%A1nchez" class="mw-redirect" title="Óscar Arias Sánchez">Óscar Arias Sánchez</a> admitted the action was a response to economic exigency.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> In response, the PRC built a new, $100 million, state-of-the-art <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">football</a> <a href="/wiki/Estadio_Nacional_de_Costa_Rica_(2011)" title="Estadio Nacional de Costa Rica (2011)">stadium in Parque la Sabana</a>, in the province of San José. Approximately 600 Chinese engineers and laborers took part in this project, and it was inaugurated in March 2011, with a match between the national teams of <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rica_national_football_team" title="Costa Rica national football team">Costa Rica</a> and <a href="/wiki/China_national_football_team" title="China national football team">China</a>. </p>
      <p>Costa Rica finished a term on the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council" title="United Nations Security Council">United Nations Security Council</a>, having been elected for a nonrenewable, two-year term in the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_election,_2007" class="mw-redirect" title="United Nations Security Council election, 2007">2007 election</a>. Its term expired on 31 December 2009; this was Costa Rica's third time on the Security Council. Elayne Whyte Gómez is the Permanent Representative of Costa Rica to the UN Office at Geneva (2017) and President of the United Nations Conference to Negotiate a Legally Binding Instrument to Prohibit Nuclear Weapons.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pacifism">Pacifism</span></h3> 
      <p>On December 1, 1948, Costa Rica abolished its military force.<sup id="cite_ref-peace_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-peace-43">[43]</a></sup> In 1949, the abolition of the military was introduced in <a href="/wiki/Article_12_of_the_Constitution_of_Costa_Rica" title="Article 12 of the Constitution of Costa Rica">Article 12</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rican_Constitution" class="mw-redirect" title="Costa Rican Constitution">Costa Rican Constitution</a>. The budget previously dedicated to the military is now dedicated to providing health care services and education.<sup id="cite_ref-nytimes-military_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes-military-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> According to DW, "Costa Rica is known for its stable democracy, progressive social policies, such as free, compulsory public education, high social well-being, and emphasis on environmental protection."<sup id="cite_ref-pacifism_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pacifism-44">[44]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2017, Costa Rica signed the UN <a href="/wiki/Treaty_on_the_Prohibition_of_Nuclear_Weapons" title="Treaty on the Prohibition of Nuclear Weapons">treaty on the Prohibition of Nuclear Weapons</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Costa_Rica" title="Demographics of Costa Rica">Demographics of Costa Rica</a>
      </div>
